<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Land</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Earth's dry surface</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Dry land" redirects here. For the song, see <a href="/wiki/Dry_Land" title="Dry Land">Dry Land</a>. For the 2010 film, see <a href="/wiki/The_Dry_Land" title="The Dry Land">The Dry Land</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For land on astronomical objects, see <a href="/wiki/Planetary_surface" title="Planetary surface">Planetary surface</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Land_(disambiguation)" title="Land (disambiguation)">Land (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coastline_as_seen_from_Chimney_Rock,_Point_Reyes_National_Seashore.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3382" data-file-width="5411" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Coastline_as_seen_from_Chimney_Rock%2C_Point_Reyes_National_Seashore.jpg/300px-Coastline_as_seen_from_Chimney_Rock%2C_Point_Reyes_National_Seashore.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Coastline_as_seen_from_Chimney_Rock%2C_Point_Reyes_National_Seashore.jpg/450px-Coastline_as_seen_from_Chimney_Rock%2C_Point_Reyes_National_Seashore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Coastline_as_seen_from_Chimney_Rock%2C_Point_Reyes_National_Seashore.jpg/600px-Coastline_as_seen_from_Chimney_Rock%2C_Point_Reyes_National_Seashore.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coastline_as_seen_from_Chimney_Rock,_Point_Reyes_National_Seashore.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Land between bodies of water at <a href="/wiki/Point_Reyes_National_Seashore" title="Point Reyes National Seashore">Point Reyes National Seashore</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Land</b>, also called <b>ground</b> or <b>dry land</b>, is the <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a>, terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Planetary_surface" title="Planetary surface">surface</a> of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> that is not submerged in the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">ocean</a> or another <a href="/wiki/Body_of_water" title="Body of water">body of water</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> It makes up 29% of Earth's surface and includes the <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a> and a variety of small <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">islands</a>. The zone where land meets sea or lakes is known as the <a href="/wiki/Coast" title="Coast">coast</a>. Land is an important part of Earth's climate system and plays important roles in the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle" title="Nitrogen cycle">nitrogen cycle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Though modern terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_plant" title="Terrestrial plant">plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_animal" title="Terrestrial animal">animals</a> evolved from aquatic creatures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Earth's <a href="/wiki/Earliest_known_life_forms" title="Earliest known life forms">first cellular life</a> likely originated on land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Several scientific disciplines study land, including <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>. One-third of land is covered in trees, 15% in crops, and a tenth in permanent snow and glaciers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-6">[6]</a></sup> Earth's land surface is almost entirely covered by <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">regolith</a>, a surface layer of rock, <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> and minerals that forms the outer part of <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 222">: 222 </span></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">terrain</a> varies greatly and consists of mountains, deserts, plains, plateaus, glaciers, and other <a href="/wiki/Landform" title="Landform">landforms</a>. In physical geology, land is divided into two major categories: relatively flat interiors called <a href="/wiki/Craton" title="Craton">cratons</a>, and mountain belts, which are formed over millions of years through <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 57">: 57 </span></sup> A major part of Earth's <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stream" title="Stream">streams</a> shape the landscape, carve rocks, transport sediments, and replenish groundwater. At high elevations or <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitudes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snow</a> is compacted and recrystallized over hundreds or thousands of years to form <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a>, which can be so heavy that they warp the Earth's crust. About 30% of land has a dry climate, due to losing more water through evaporation than it gains from <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-7">[7]</a></sup> Since warm air rises, this generates winds, though the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth's rotation">rotation</a> and uneven sun distribution also play a part.
</p><p>The zone where land meets sea or lakes is known as the <a href="/wiki/Coast" title="Coast">coast</a>. Various natural boundaries can mark this separation, whether cliffs, solid rock landforms, marshes, or swamps. Since coastlines often change with the tide, the coast's <a href="/wiki/Baseline_(sea)" title="Baseline (sea)">baseline</a> is the legal demarcation used to determine <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone" title="Exclusive economic zone">exclusive economic zones</a>; often, the low-water line is used as the baseline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_animal" title="Terrestrial animal">Life on land</a> depends on <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a> from rivers, streams, lakes, and glaciers, which constitute only 3% of the water on Earth. Prehistoric humans are thought to have settled near rivers;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> more recently, Neolithic civilizations were also concentrated near rivers for hydration and agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The vast majority of human activity throughout history has occurred in land areas that support <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a>, and various <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resources</a>. Today, 95% of the world's population lives on 10% of the land,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> although three-quarters of ice-free land is altered by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">Land use</a> refers to the human allocation of land for various purposes, including farming, ranching, and recreation (e.g. national parks);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> one-third of land is used for agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbankagri_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-worldbankagri-14">[14]</a></sup> In recent decades, scientists and policymakers have emphasizes the need to <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_land_management" title="Sustainable land management">manage</a> land and its biosphere more sustainably, notably by restoring <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">degraded soil</a>, preserving <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>, protecting <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered species</a>, and addressing <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Land is commonly defined as "the solid, dry surface of the Earth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-1">[1]</a></sup> However, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Governments" title="Governments">governments</a> and modern scholars have adopted a more integrative view, and also use the term <i>land</i> to refer to not only the physical cover (<a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sand" title="Sand">sand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a>), but also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rivers" title="Rivers">rivers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wetlands" title="Wetlands">wetlands</a>, shallow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lakes" title="Lakes">lakes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_resources" title="Natural resources">natural resources</a>, non-marine <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">fauna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">flora</a> (<a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>), <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">the near-surface air and climate above dry land</a>, <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> reserves, and the physical results of human activity on land, such as <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Land#Land_cover" title="Land">Land cover</a> refers to the material physically present on the land surface, for example woody crops, herbaceous crops, barren land, shrub-covered areas. Artificial surfaces (including cities) account for about a third of one per cent of all land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">Land use</a> refers to human allocation of land for various purposes, including farming, ranching, and recreation (e.g. national parks); worldwide, there are an estimated 16.7 million km<sup>2</sup> (6.4 million sq mi) of cropland, and 33.5 million km<sup>2</sup> (12.9 million sq mi) of pastureland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hooke2012_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hooke2012-16">[16]</a></sup> In economics, <a href="/wiki/Land_(economics)" title="Land (economics)"><i>land</i></a> refers to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Factor_of_production" title="Factor of production">factor of production</a>. It can be leased in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Renting" title="Renting">rent</a>, and its various resources (trees, oil, metals) can be used as <a href="/wiki/Raw_material" title="Raw material">raw material</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>A continuous area of land surrounded by an ocean is called a <a href="/wiki/Landmass" title="Landmass">landmass</a>. Although it may be most often written as one word to distinguish it from the usage "land mass"—the measure of land area—it is also used as two words. Landmasses include <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">supercontinents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">islands</a>. There are four major continuous landmasses on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>: <a href="/wiki/Afro-Eurasia" title="Afro-Eurasia">Afro-Eurasia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia</a>. Land capable of being <a href="/wiki/Plough" title="Plough">ploughed</a> and used to grow <a href="/wiki/Crop" title="Crop">crops</a>, is called <i><a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word <i>land</i> is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <span title="Old English (ca. 450-1100)-language text"><i lang="ang">land</i></span> 'ground, soil', also 'definite portion of the earth's surface, home region of a person or a people, territory marked by political boundaries'. It evolved from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a> <span title="Proto-Germanic-language text">*<i lang="gem">landą</i></span> and from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a> <span title="Proto-Indo-European-language text">*<i lang="ine">lendʰ-</i></span> 'land, open land, heath'. The word has many <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognates</a> in other languages, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Norse_language" title="Old Norse language">Old Norse</a>: <i lang="non">land</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Frisian_language" title="Old Frisian language">Old Frisian</a>: <i lang="ofs">land</i>, <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a>: <span lang="got">land</span>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de">Land</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Irish_language" title="Old Irish language">Old Irish</a>: <i lang="sga">land</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Welsh_language" title="Middle Welsh language">Middle Welsh</a>: <i lang="wlm">llan</i> 'an open space', <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>: <i lang="cy">llan</i> 'enclosure, church', <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a>: <i lang="br">lann</i> 'heath', <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_Slavonic_language" title="Church Slavonic language">Church Slavonic</a>: <span lang="cu">ledina</span> 'wasteland, heath', and <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>: <i lang="cs">lada</i> 'fallow land'. Etymological evidence within Gothic usage suggests that the original meaning of <i>land</i> was 'a definite portion of the earth's surface owned by an individual or home of a nation.' The meaning was extended to 'solid surface of the earth'. The original meaning is now associated with <i><a href="/wiki/Country" title="Country">country</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Country" title="Country">country</a> or region may be referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Homeland#Motherland" title="Homeland">motherland</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatherland" title="Fatherland">fatherland</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Homeland" title="Homeland">homeland</a> of its people. Many countries and other places have names incorporating the suffix, <a href="/wiki/-land" title="-land">-land</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swaziland" title="Swaziland">Swaziland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian</a> suffix <i><a href="/wiki/-stan" title="-stan">-stan</a></i>, ultimately descended from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Iranian_language" title="Proto-Indo-Iranian language">Proto-Indo-Iranian</a> <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">*<i>sthāna-</i></span></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-20">[20]</a></sup> is analogous to the suffix <i><a href="/wiki/-land" title="-land">-land</a></i>, and is present in many country and location names, such as <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>. The suffix is also used more generally, as in Persian <span title="Persian-language romanization"><i lang="fa-Latn">rigestân</i></span> (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">ریگستان</span></span>) "place of sand, desert", <span title="Persian-language romanization"><i lang="fa-Latn">golestân</i></span> (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">گلستان</span></span>) "place of flowers, garden", <span title="Irish-language romanization"><i lang="ga-Latn">gurestân</i></span> (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">گورستان</span></span>) "graveyard, cemetery", <i>Hindustân</i> (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">هندوستان</span></span>) "land of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indo</a> people".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Delineation">Delineation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Delineation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Continents">Continents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Continents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">Continent</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Continental_models.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="755" data-file-width="1488" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Continental_models.gif/330px-Continental_models.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Continental_models.gif/495px-Continental_models.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Continental_models.gif/660px-Continental_models.gif 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Continental_models.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animated map showing the world's continents according to different models.</div></div></div>
<p>Land is separated by bodies of water. Apart from small islands, land is made up of four <a href="/wiki/Landmass" title="Landmass">landmasses</a>, from largest to smallest: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afroeurasia" title="Afroeurasia">Afro-Eurasia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mainland_Australia" title="Mainland Australia">Australia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McColl2_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-McColl2-21">[21]</a></sup> which are then grouped into <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a>. Up to seven geographical regions are commonly regarded as continents. Ordered from largest in area to smallest, these seven regions are: <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> There are between five and seven continents, depending on how they are grouped. Some group North America and South America together as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/America" title="America">America</a>; Europe and Asia can also be grouped as <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatlGeo_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatlGeo-24">[24]</a></sup> or combined with Africa into <a href="/wiki/Afro-Eurasia" title="Afro-Eurasia">Afro-Eurasia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McColl2_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-McColl2-21">[21]</a></sup> Others do not count Antarctica as a continent. Most of the island countries and territories of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> are frequently grouped with Australia to form a geographical region called <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Borders">Borders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Borders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Border" title="Border">Border</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Panmunjeom_DMZ.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2054" data-file-width="3467" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Panmunjeom_DMZ.png/220px-Panmunjeom_DMZ.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Panmunjeom_DMZ.png/330px-Panmunjeom_DMZ.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Panmunjeom_DMZ.png/440px-Panmunjeom_DMZ.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Panmunjeom_DMZ.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>South Korean policemen standing guard at the North Korea-South Korea border. View from South Korea.</div></div></div>
<p>Borders are <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geographical</a> boundaries, imposed either by geographic features (<a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mountain_range" title="Mountain range">mountain ranges</a>, <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a>) or by <a href="/wiki/Polity" title="Polity">political entities</a> (<a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">governments</a>, states, or subnational entities). Political borders can be established through <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">warfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colonization" title="Colonization">colonization</a>, or mutual agreements between the political entities that reside in those areas;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> the creation of these agreements is called <a href="/wiki/Boundary_delimitation" title="Boundary delimitation">boundary delimitation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some borders—including most states' internal administrative borders, or inter-state borders within the <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Area" title="Schengen Area">Schengen Area</a>—are <a href="/wiki/Open_border" title="Open border">open</a> and completely unguarded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Most political borders are partially or fully controlled, and may be crossed legally only at designated <a href="/wiki/Border_checkpoint" title="Border checkpoint">border checkpoints</a>; adjacent <a href="/wiki/Border_control#Border_zones" title="Border control">border zones</a> may also be controlled. <a href="/wiki/Buffer_zone" title="Buffer zone">Buffer zones</a> may be setup on borders between belligerent entities to lower the risk of escalation.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Earth" title="History of Earth">History of Earth</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth_formation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Earth_formation.jpg/200px-Earth_formation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Earth_formation.jpg/300px-Earth_formation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Earth_formation.jpg/400px-Earth_formation.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earth_formation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's conception of <a href="/wiki/Hadean" title="Hadean">Hadean Eon</a> Earth</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest material found in the Solar System is dated to <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7017144129870720000♠"></span>4.5672<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.0006 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Billion_years_ago" title="Billion years ago">bya</a></span> (billion years ago);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bowring_housch1995_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-bowring_housch1995-30">[30]</a></sup> therefore, the Earth itself must have been formed by <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a> around this time. The <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">formation and evolution of the Solar System</a> bodies occurred in tandem with the Sun. In theory, a <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System#Formation_of_the_planets" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">solar nebula</a> partitions a volume out of a <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular cloud</a> by gravitational collapse, which begins to spin and flatten into a <a href="/wiki/Circumstellar_disc" title="Circumstellar disc">circumstellar disc</a>, out of which the planets then grow (in tandem with the star). A nebula contains gas, ice grains and <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">dust</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial nuclides</a>). In <a href="/wiki/Nebular_hypothesis" title="Nebular hypothesis">nebular theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimals</a> commence forming as <a href="/wiki/Granular_material#Granular_gases" title="Granular material">particulate</a> matter accrues by <a href="/wiki/Cohesion_(geology)" title="Cohesion (geology)">cohesive clumping</a> and then by gravity. The assembly of the primordial Earth proceeded for 10–<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7014631152000000000♠"></span>20 <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">myr</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature418_6901_949_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature418_6901_949-31">[31]</a></sup> By <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7017143271504000000♠"></span>4.54<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.04 bya</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> the primordial Earth had formed.
</p><p>Earth's atmosphere and oceans were formed by <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic</a> activity and <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">outgassing</a> that included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_vapour" title="Water vapour">water vapour</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origin_of_the_world%27s_oceans" title="Origin of the world's oceans">origin of the world's oceans</a> was condensation augmented by water and ice delivered by <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protoplanet" title="Protoplanet">proto-planets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watersource_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-watersource-33">[33]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Faint_young_Sun_paradox" title="Faint young Sun paradox">this model</a>, atmospheric "<a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>" kept the oceans from freezing while the newly forming Sun was only at 70% <a href="/wiki/Solar_luminosity" title="Solar luminosity">luminosity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asp2002_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-asp2002-34">[34]</a></sup> By <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7017110451600000000♠"></span>3.5 bya</span>, the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a> was established, which helped prevent the atmosphere from being stripped away by the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physorg20100304_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-physorg20100304-35">[35]</a></sup> The atmosphere and oceans of the Earth continuously shape the land by eroding and transporting solids on the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a>, which currently forms the Earth's land, was created when the molten outer layer of the planet Earth cooled to <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">form a solid mass</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> as the accumulated water vapour began to act in the atmosphere. Once land became capable of supporting life, biodiversity evolved over hundreds of million years, expanding continually except when punctuated by mass extinctions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFerry2010_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFerry2010-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The two models<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williams_santosh2004_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-williams_santosh2004-39">[39]</a></sup> that explain land mass propose either a steady growth to the present-day forms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science164_1229_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-science164_1229-40">[40]</a></sup> or, more likely, a rapid growth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tp322_19_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-tp322_19-41">[41]</a></sup> early in Earth history<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rg6_175_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-rg6_175-42">[42]</a></sup> followed by a long-term steady continental area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science310_5754_1671_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-science310_5754_1671-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jaes23_799_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-jaes23_799-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ajes38_613_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-ajes38_613-45">[45]</a></sup> Continents are formed by <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>, a process ultimately driven by the continuous loss of heat from the Earth's interior. On <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">time scales</a> lasting hundreds of millions of years, the <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">supercontinents</a> have formed and broken apart three times. Roughly <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7016236682000000000♠"></span>750 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" title="Mya (unit)">mya</a></span> (million years ago), one of the earliest known supercontinents, <a href="/wiki/Rodinia" title="Rodinia">Rodinia</a>, began to break apart. The continents later recombined to form <a href="/wiki/Pannotia" title="Pannotia">Pannotia</a>, 600–<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7016170411040000000♠"></span>540 mya</span>, then finally <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>, which also broke apart <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7015568036800000000♠"></span>180 mya</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-as92_324_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-as92_324-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terrain">Terrain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Terrain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">Terrain</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maps-for-free_Sierra_Nevada.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="497" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Maps-for-free_Sierra_Nevada.png/220px-Maps-for-free_Sierra_Nevada.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Maps-for-free_Sierra_Nevada.png/330px-Maps-for-free_Sierra_Nevada.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Maps-for-free_Sierra_Nevada.png/440px-Maps-for-free_Sierra_Nevada.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maps-for-free_Sierra_Nevada.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Relief map of <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_(Spain)" title="Sierra Nevada (Spain)">Sierra Nevada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>. Elevated areas are <a href="/wiki/False_color" title="False color">colored</a> in red, and low areas in green.</div></div></div>
<p>Terrain can refer to an area of land, or to its <a href="/wiki/Landform" title="Landform">landforms</a> (for example, mountains). The study of a terrain's shape and landforms is called <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a>, while the scientific study of the origins and evolution of these landforms is called <a href="/wiki/Geomorphology" title="Geomorphology">geomorphology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">Cartography</a> refers to the practice of mapmaking. Terrain affects surface <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_flow" title="Water flow">water flow</a> and distribution. Over a large area, it can affect <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> patterns. The terrain of a region largely determines its suitability for human settlement: flatter <a href="/wiki/Alluvial_plain" title="Alluvial plain">alluvial plains</a> tend to have better farming soils than steeper, rockier uplands.
</p>
<h4><span id="Altitude.2C_elevation.2C_and_relief"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Altitude,_elevation,_and_relief">Altitude, elevation, and relief</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Altitude, elevation, and relief">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Elevation is defined as the vertical distance between an object and sea level, while altitude is defined as the vertical distance from an object to Earth's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The elevation of Earth's land surface varies from the low point of −418 m (−1,371 ft) at the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">Dead Sea</a>, to a maximum altitude of 8,848 m (29,029 ft) at the top of Mount Everest. The mean height of land above sea level is about 797 m (2,615 ft),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> with 98.9% of dry land situated above <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Relief refers to the difference in elevation within a landscape; for example, flat terrain would have "low relief", while terrain with a large elevation difference between the highest and lowest points would be deemed "high relief". Most land has relatively low relief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The change in elevation between two points of the terrain is called a slope or gradient. A <a href="/wiki/Topographic_map" title="Topographic map">topographic map</a> is a form of <a href="/wiki/Terrain_cartography" title="Terrain cartography">terrain cartography</a> which depicts terrain in terms of its elevation, slope, and the orientation of its landforms. It has prominent <a href="/wiki/Contour_line" title="Contour line">contour lines</a>, which connect points of similar elevation, while perpendicular slope lines point in the direction of the steepest slope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hypsometric_tints" title="Hypsometric tints">Hypsometric tints</a> are colors placed between contour lines to indicate elevation relative to <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hikers" title="Hikers">Hikers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_climbers" title="Rock climbers">rock climbers</a> must be careful to not ascend land too rapidly or else they may get <a href="/wiki/Altitude_sickness" title="Altitude sickness">altitude sickness</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Highlands_and_lowlands">Highlands and lowlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Highlands and lowlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A contrast between highlands, or uplands, and lowlands is drawn in several earth science fields. In river ecology, "upland" rivers are fast-moving and colder than "lowland" rivers, encouraging different species of fish and other aquatic wildlife to live in these habitats. In addition, the term <a href="/wiki/Moorland" title="Moorland">moorland</a> refers to upland shrubland biomes with acidic soils, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heathland" title="Heathland">heathlands</a> are lowland shrublands with acidic soils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Layers">Layers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Layers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Pedosphere" title="Pedosphere">pedosphere</a> is the outermost layer of Earth's continental surface and is composed of soil and subject to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pedogenesis" title="Pedogenesis">soil formation processes</a>. Below it, the <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a> encompasses both Earth's crust and the uppermost layer of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_mantle" title="Earth's mantle">mantle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The lithosphere "floats" on top of the mantle below it via <a href="/wiki/Isostasy" title="Isostasy">isostasy</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_cover">Land cover</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Land cover">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Land_cover" title="Land cover">Land cover</a> and <a href="/wiki/Land_cover_mapping" title="Land cover mapping">Land cover mapping</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Land_cover_IGBP.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="3600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Land_cover_IGBP.png/330px-Land_cover_IGBP.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Land_cover_IGBP.png/495px-Land_cover_IGBP.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Land_cover_IGBP.png/660px-Land_cover_IGBP.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Land_cover_IGBP.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Land cover as classified by the <a href="/wiki/International_Geosphere-Biosphere_Programme" title="International Geosphere-Biosphere Programme">International Geosphere-Biosphere Programme</a> (IGBP) into 17 classes</div></div></div>
<p>Land cover is the physical material at the surface of the earth. Among many things, it includes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grass" title="Grass">grass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">asphalt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">trees</a>, bare ground, <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>. Earth cover is the expression used by ecologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederick_Edward_Clements" title="Frederick Edward Clements">Frederick Edward Clements</a> that has its closest modern equivalent being <a href="/wiki/Vegetation" title="Vegetation">vegetation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MAB_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-MAB-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 52">: 52 </span></sup> The expression continues to be used by the United States <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Land_Management" title="Bureau of Land Management">Bureau of Land Management</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two primary methods for capturing information on land cover: field survey and analysis of <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remotely sensed imagery</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_change_modelling" title="Land change modelling">Land change models</a> can be built from these types of data to assess changes in land cover over time. One of the major land cover issues (as with all <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resource</a> inventories) is that every survey defines similarly named categories in different ways. For instance, there are many definitions of "<a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forest</a>"—sometimes within the same organisation—that may or may not incorporate various forest features (e.g., stand height, canopy cover, strip width, the inclusion of grasses, and rates of growth for <a href="/wiki/Wood_production" title="Wood production">timber production</a>). Areas without trees may be classified as <a href="/wiki/Forest_cover" title="Forest cover">forest cover</a> "if the intention is to re-plant" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK" title="UK">UK</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>), while areas with many trees may not be labelled as forest "if the trees are not growing fast enough" (<a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>).
</p><p>Land cover change detection using remote sensing and geospatial data provides baseline information for assessing the climate change impacts on habitats and biodiversity, as well as natural resources, in the target areas. Land cover change detection and mapping is a key component of interdisciplinary <a href="/wiki/Land_change_science" title="Land change science">land change science</a>, which uses it to determine the consequences of land change on climate.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Soil">Soil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Soil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">Soil</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Estructura-suelo.jpg"><img alt="This is a diagram and related photograph of soil layers from bedrock to soil." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="541" data-file-width="513" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Estructura-suelo.jpg/220px-Estructura-suelo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Estructura-suelo.jpg/330px-Estructura-suelo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Estructura-suelo.jpg/440px-Estructura-suelo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Estructura-suelo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A, B, and C represent the <a href="/wiki/Soil_horizon" title="Soil horizon">soil profile</a>, a notation firstly coined by <a href="/wiki/Vasily_Dokuchaev" title="Vasily Dokuchaev">Vasily Dokuchaev</a> (1846–1903), the father of pedology. Here, A is the <a href="/wiki/Topsoil" title="Topsoil">topsoil</a>; B is a <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">regolith</a>; C is a <a href="/wiki/Saprolite" title="Saprolite">saprolite</a> (a less-weathered regolith); the bottom-most layer represents the <a href="/wiki/Bedrock" title="Bedrock">bedrock</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Soil, also known as earth, <a href="/wiki/Dirt" title="Dirt">dirt</a>, or <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/pedolith" title="wiktionary:pedolith">pedolith</a><i>,</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Mixture" title="Mixture">mixture</a> of <a href="/wiki/Organic_matter" title="Organic matter">organic matter</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minerals" title="Minerals">minerals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> that together support <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>. Soil consists of a solid phase of minerals and organic matter (the soil matrix), as well as a <a href="/wiki/Porosity" title="Porosity">porous</a> phase that holds <a href="/wiki/Soil_gas" title="Soil gas">gases</a> (the soil atmosphere) and <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> (the soil solution).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Accordingly, soil is a three-<a href="/wiki/State_of_matter" title="State of matter">state</a> system of solids, liquids, and gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Soil is a product of several factors: the influence of <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">relief</a> (elevation, orientation, and slope of terrain), organisms, and the soil's <a href="/wiki/Parent_material" title="Parent material">parent materials</a> (original minerals) interacting over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilluly1975_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilluly1975-60">[60]</a></sup> It continually undergoes development by way of numerous physical, chemical and biological processes, which include <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> with associated <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>.
</p><p>Given its complexity and strong internal <a href="/wiki/Connectedness" title="Connectedness">connectedness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_ecology" title="Soil ecology">soil ecologists</a> regard soil as an <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Soil acts as an engineering medium, a habitat for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soil_organisms" title="Soil organisms">soil organisms</a>, a recycling system for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutrients" title="Nutrients">nutrients</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_waste" title="Organic waste">organic wastes</a>, a regulator of <a href="/wiki/Water_quality" title="Water quality">water quality</a>, a modifier of <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_chemistry" title="Atmospheric chemistry">atmospheric composition</a>, and a medium for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_growth" title="Plant growth">plant growth</a>, making it a critically important provider of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Since soil has a tremendous range of available <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">niches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a>, it contains a prominent part of the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a>. A gram of soil can contain billions of organisms, belonging to thousands of species, mostly microbial and largely still unexplored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soil is a major component of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a>. The world's ecosystems are impacted in far-reaching ways by the processes carried out in the soil, with effects ranging from <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">ozone depletion</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainforest_destruction" title="Rainforest destruction">rainforest destruction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>. With respect to Earth's <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>, soil acts as an important <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">carbon reservoir</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> and it is potentially one of the most reactive to human disturbance<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> and climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davidson_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson-67">[67]</a></sup> As the planet warms, it has been predicted that soils will add carbon dioxide to the atmosphere due to increased <a href="/wiki/Soil_biology" title="Soil biology">biological</a> activity at higher temperatures, a <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a> (amplification).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> This prediction has, however, been questioned on consideration of more recent knowledge on soil carbon turnover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Continental_crust">Continental crust</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Continental crust">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Continental_crust" title="Continental crust">Continental crust</a> and <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">Continental shelf</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">Abundance of elements in Earth's crust</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Topo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="636" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Topo.jpg/290px-Topo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Topo.jpg/435px-Topo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Topo.jpg/580px-Topo.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Topo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The thickness of <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)#Earth's_crust" title="Crust (geology)">Earth's crust</a> (km)</div></div></div>
<p>Continental crust is the layer of <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic rocks</a> that forms the geological <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a> and the areas of shallow seabed close to their shores, known as <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelves</a>. This layer is sometimes called <i><a href="/wiki/Sial" title="Sial">sial</a></i> because its bulk composition is richer in <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> silicates (Al-Si) and has a lower density compared to the <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_crust" title="Oceanic crust">oceanic crust</a>, called <i><a href="/wiki/Sima_(geology)" title="Sima (geology)">sima</a></i> which is richer in <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> silicate (Mg-Si) minerals. Changes in <a href="/wiki/Seismic_wave" title="Seismic wave">seismic wave</a> velocities have shown that at a certain depth (the <a href="/wiki/Conrad_discontinuity" title="Conrad discontinuity">Conrad discontinuity</a>), there is a reasonably sharp contrast between the more <a href="/wiki/Felsic" title="Felsic">felsic</a> upper continental crust and the lower continental crust, which is more <a href="/wiki/Mafic" title="Mafic">mafic</a> in character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>The composition of land is not uniform across the Earth, varying between locations and between <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">strata</a> within the same location. The most prominent components of upper continental crust include <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">aluminium oxide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-71">[71]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Continental_crust" title="Continental crust">continental crust</a> consists of lower density material such as the igneous rocks <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andesite" title="Andesite">andesite</a>. Less common is <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a>, a denser volcanic rock that is the primary constituent of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocean_floor" title="Ocean floor">ocean floors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-layers_earth_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-layers_earth-72">[72]</a></sup> Sedimentary rock is formed from the accumulation of sediment that becomes buried and <a href="/wiki/Diagenesis" title="Diagenesis">compacted together</a>. Nearly 75% of the continental surfaces are covered by sedimentary rocks, although they form about 5% of the crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jessey_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-jessey-73">[73]</a></sup> The third form of rock material found on Earth is <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic rock</a>, which is created from the transformation of pre-existing rock types through high pressures, high temperatures, or both. The most abundant <a href="/wiki/Silicate_mineral" title="Silicate mineral">silicate minerals</a> on Earth's surface include <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feldspar" title="Feldspar">feldspars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibole" title="Amphibole">amphibole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mica" title="Mica">mica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">pyroxene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_pater_lissauer2010_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_pater_lissauer2010-74">[74]</a></sup> Common <a href="/wiki/Carbonate_mineral" title="Carbonate mineral">carbonate minerals</a> include <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a> (found in <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Dolomite_(mineral)" title="Dolomite (mineral)">dolomite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wekn_bulakh2004_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-wekn_bulakh2004-75">[75]</a></sup> The rock that makes up land is thicker than <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_crust" title="Oceanic crust">oceanic crust</a>, and it is far more varied in terms of composition. About 31% of this continental crust is submerged in shallow water, forming continental shelves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geomorphology">Geomorphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Geomorphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Erosion_and_tectonics" title="Erosion and tectonics">Erosion and tectonics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Types_of_volcanic_eruptions" title="Types of volcanic eruptions">volcanic eruptions</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flooding" title="Flooding">flooding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciation" title="Glaciation">glaciation</a>, the growth of <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a>, and meteorite impacts are among the processes that constantly reshape Earth's surface over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological_time" title="Geological time">geological time</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kring_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-kring-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Erosion_and_weathering">Erosion and weathering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Erosion and weathering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Erosion transports one part of land to another via natural processes, such as <a href="/wiki/Aeolian_processes" title="Aeolian processes">wind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>. In contrast, weathering wears away rock and other solid land without transporting the land somewhere else.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plate_tectonics">Plate tectonics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Plate tectonics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">Plate tectonics</a> refers to the theory that Earth's crust is divided into "plates" that move along the mantle via <a href="/wiki/Continental_drift" title="Continental drift">continental drift</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The place where two plates meet is called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plate_boundary" title="Plate boundary">plate boundary</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earthquakes" title="Earthquakes">Earthquakes</a> and volcanic activity are common at these boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Volcanic_activity">Volcanic activity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Volcanic activity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Volcanic activity refers to any rupture in Earth's surface where <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> escapes, therefore becoming <a href="/wiki/Lava" title="Lava">lava</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Landforms">Landforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Landforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coasts">Coasts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Coasts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The area where land meets the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">ocean</a> or another large body of water like a <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lake</a> is called a <a href="/wiki/Coast" title="Coast">coast</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> or, alternatively, a "coastline".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> has around 620,000 kilometres (390,000 mi) of coastline. When land is in contact with bodies of water, the land is likely <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathered</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">eroded</a>. The precise weathering of a coastline may be impacted by the <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a>, caused by changes in gravitational forces on larger bodies of water.
</p><p>Coasts are important zones in natural <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, often home to a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>. On land, they harbour important ecosystems such as freshwater or estuarine <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a>, which are important for bird populations and other terrestrial animals. In wave-protected areas they harbor <a href="/wiki/Salt_marsh" title="Salt marsh">saltmarshes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">mangroves</a> or <a href="/wiki/Seagrass_meadow" title="Seagrass meadow">seagrasses</a>, all of which can provide <a href="/wiki/Nursery_habitat" title="Nursery habitat">nursery habitat</a> for finfish, <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a>, and other aquatic species. Rocky shores are usually found along exposed coasts and provide habitat for a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Sessility_(motility)" title="Sessility (motility)">sessile animals</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">mussels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Starfish" title="Starfish">starfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barnacle" title="Barnacle">barnacles</a>) and various kinds of <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweeds</a>. Along <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> coasts with clear, nutrient-poor water, <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a> can often be found between depths of 1–50 meters (3.3–164.0 feet).
</p><p>According to a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> atlas, 44% of all people live within 150 km (93 mi) of the sea. Because of their importance in society and high concentration of population, the coast is important for major parts of the global food and economic system, and they provide many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a> to humankind. For example, important human activities happen in <a href="/wiki/Port" title="Port">port cities</a>. Coastal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fisheries" title="Fisheries">fisheries</a> (commercial, recreational, and subsistence) and <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a> are major economic activities and create jobs, livelihoods, and <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> for the majority of coastal human populations. Other coastal spaces like <a href="/wiki/Beach" title="Beach">beaches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seaside_resort" title="Seaside resort">seaside resorts</a> generate large revenues through <a href="/wiki/Tourism" title="Tourism">tourism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Marine_coastal_ecosystem" title="Marine coastal ecosystem">Marine coastal ecosystems</a> can also provide protection against <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level rise</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunamis</a>. In many countries, <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">mangroves</a> are the primary source of wood for fuel (e.g. charcoal) and building material. Coastal ecosystems like mangroves and seagrasses have a much higher capacity for <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sequestration" title="Carbon sequestration">carbon sequestration</a> than many <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_ecosystem" title="Terrestrial ecosystem">terrestrial ecosystems</a>, and as such can play a critical role in the near future to help <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">mitigate climate change</a> effects by uptake of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">atmospheric anthropogenic carbon dioxide</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mountains">Mountains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Mountains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Any elevated part of Earth's crust can be called a mountain. They are formed from <a href="/wiki/Orogeny" title="Orogeny">orogeny</a> events, where a plate at a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convergent_plate_boundary" title="Convergent plate boundary">convergent plate boundary</a> pushes one plate above the other.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plateaus">Plateaus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Plateaus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plateau" title="Plateau">Plateau</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kukenan_Tepuy_at_Sunset.jpg"><img alt="Kukenan Tepuy in Gran Sabana National Park, Venezuela" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Kukenan_Tepuy_at_Sunset.jpg/220px-Kukenan_Tepuy_at_Sunset.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Kukenan_Tepuy_at_Sunset.jpg/330px-Kukenan_Tepuy_at_Sunset.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Kukenan_Tepuy_at_Sunset.jpg/440px-Kukenan_Tepuy_at_Sunset.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kukenan_Tepuy_at_Sunset.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kuken%C3%A1n-tepui" title="Kukenán-tepui">Kukenan Tepuy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Canaima_National_Park" title="Canaima National Park">Gran Sabana National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a></div></div></div>
<p>A plateau, also called a high plain or a tableland, is an area of a <a href="/wiki/Highland" title="Highland">highland</a> consisting of flat <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">terrain</a> that is raised sharply above the surrounding area on at least one side. Often one or more sides have deep hills or <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">escarpments</a>. Plateaus can be formed by a number of processes, including <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanic" title="Volcanic">volcanic</a> <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extrusion" title="Extrusion">extrusion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lava" title="Lava">lava</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a> by water and <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a>. Plateaus are classified according to their surrounding environment as intermontane, piedmont, or continental. A few plateaus may have a small flat top while others have wide ones.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biomes">Biomes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Biomes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">Biome</a></div>
<p>A biome is an area "characterized by its vegetation, soil, climate, and <a href="/wiki/Wildlife" title="Wildlife">wildlife</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-82">[82]</a></sup> There are five major types of biomes: grasslands, forests, deserts, tundras, as well as aquatic biomes (marine and freshwater).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-82">[82]</a></sup> Biomes may contain a variety of <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, which refer to the interaction between organisms within an environment. A <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a> is an even narrower term, referring to the environment where a given species lives. Biomes may span more than one continent, and contain a variety of ecosystems and habitats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Desert">Desert</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Desert">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">Desert</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:White_Desert,_Rock_formations,_Egypt.jpg"><img alt="A picture of the White Desert in Egypt, with cliffs, dunes, and white chalk rock formations created through erosion by wind and sand" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/White_Desert%2C_Rock_formations%2C_Egypt.jpg/220px-White_Desert%2C_Rock_formations%2C_Egypt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/White_Desert%2C_Rock_formations%2C_Egypt.jpg/330px-White_Desert%2C_Rock_formations%2C_Egypt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/White_Desert%2C_Rock_formations%2C_Egypt.jpg/440px-White_Desert%2C_Rock_formations%2C_Egypt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:White_Desert,_Rock_formations,_Egypt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/White_Desert_National_Park" title="White Desert National Park">White Desert National Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a></div></div></div>
<p>A desert is a barren area of <a href="/wiki/Landscape" title="Landscape">landscape</a> where little <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a> occurs and, consequently, living conditions are hostile for plant and animal life. The lack of vegetation exposes the unprotected surface of the ground to <a href="/wiki/Denudation" title="Denudation">denudation</a>. About one-third of the land surface of the Earth is <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">semi-arid</a>. This includes much of the <a href="/wiki/Polar_regions_of_Earth" title="Polar regions of Earth">polar regions</a>, where little precipitation occurs, and which are sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Polar_desert" title="Polar desert">polar deserts</a> or "cold deserts". Deserts can be classified by the amount of precipitation that falls, by the temperature that prevails, by the causes of desertification, or by their geographical location.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tundra">Tundra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Tundra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">Tundra</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tundra_at_Adventfjord,_Svalbard.jpg"><img alt="Extensive tundra spreads at the shores of the Spitsbergen fjords. In summer, the permafrost thaws to a depth of about 1m and provides a fruitful ground for an extensive arctic flora" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3159" data-file-width="5616" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Tundra_at_Adventfjord%2C_Svalbard.jpg/220px-Tundra_at_Adventfjord%2C_Svalbard.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Tundra_at_Adventfjord%2C_Svalbard.jpg/330px-Tundra_at_Adventfjord%2C_Svalbard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Tundra_at_Adventfjord%2C_Svalbard.jpg/440px-Tundra_at_Adventfjord%2C_Svalbard.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tundra_at_Adventfjord,_Svalbard.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">Tundra</a> at <a href="/wiki/Adventfjorden" title="Adventfjorden">Adventfjorden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a></div></div></div>
<p>Tundra is a biome where tree growth is hindered by frigid temperatures and short growing seasons. The term <i>tundra</i> comes through <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> тундра (<i>tundra</i>) from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kildin_S%C3%A1mi_language" title="Kildin Sámi language">Kildin Sámi</a> word тӯндар (<i>tūndâr</i>) meaning "uplands", "treeless mountain tract". There are three regions and associated types of tundra: Arctic tundra, <a href="/wiki/Alpine_tundra" title="Alpine tundra">alpine tundra</a>, and Antarctic tundra.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Forest">Forest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Forest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">Forest</a></div>
