<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ryssebergen_2012b.jpg"><img alt="A forest in Ryssbergen, Sweden" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4928" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Ryssebergen_2012b.jpg/220px-Ryssebergen_2012b.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Ryssebergen_2012b.jpg/330px-Ryssebergen_2012b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Ryssebergen_2012b.jpg/440px-Ryssebergen_2012b.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ryssebergen_2012b.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forest</a> in Ryssbergen, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a></div></div></div>
<p>A forest is an area of land dominated by <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">trees</a>. Hundreds of definitions of forest are used throughout the world, incorporating factors such as tree density, tree height, land use, legal standing, and ecological function. The United Nations' <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> (FAO) defines a forest as, "Land spanning more than 0.5 hectares with trees higher than 5 meters and a <a href="/wiki/Canopy_(biology)" title="Canopy (biology)">canopy</a> cover of more than 10 per cent, or trees able to reach these thresholds <i>in situ</i>. It does not include land that is predominantly under agricultural or urban use." Using this definition, <i><a href="/wiki/Global_Forest_Resources_Assessment_(FRA)" title="Global Forest Resources Assessment (FRA)">Global Forest Resources Assessment</a> 2020</i> (FRA 2020) found that forests covered 4.06 billion hectares (10.0 billion acres; 40.6 million square kilometres; 15.7 million square miles), or approximately 31 per cent of the world's land area in 2020.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Grasslands">Grasslands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Grasslands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">Grassland</a></div>
<p>Grasslands are areas where the <a href="/wiki/Vegetation" title="Vegetation">vegetation</a> is dominated by grasses (<a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">Poaceae</a>). However, sedge (<a href="/wiki/Cyperaceae" title="Cyperaceae">Cyperaceae</a>) and rush (<a href="/wiki/Juncaceae" title="Juncaceae">Juncaceae</a>) can also be found along with variable proportions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legumes" title="Legumes">legumes</a>, like <a href="/wiki/Clover" title="Clover">clover</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Herbaceous_plant" title="Herbaceous plant">herbs</a>. Grasslands occur naturally on all continents except <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> and are found in most <a href="/wiki/Ecoregion" title="Ecoregion">ecoregions</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. Furthermore, grasslands are one of the largest <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biomes</a> on earth and dominate the landscape worldwide. There are different types of grasslands: natural grasslands, semi-natural grasslands, and agricultural grasslands. They cover 31–69% of the Earth's land area.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora">Flora</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Flora">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryophytes" title="Embryophytes">Embryophytes</a>, the land plants, include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bryophytes" title="Bryophytes">bryophytes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lycophytes" title="Lycophytes">lycophytes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferns" title="Ferns">ferns</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gymnosperms" title="Gymnosperms">gymnosperms</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flowering_plants" title="Flowering plants">flowering plants</a>. They are adapted for life on land, unlike <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquatic_plants" title="Aquatic plants">aquatic plants</a>, although many are semi-aquatic. All embryophytes except bryophytes and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polysporangiophytes" title="Polysporangiophytes">polysporangiophytes</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vascular_plants" title="Vascular plants">vascular plants</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fauna">Fauna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Fauna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Unlike plants, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrestrial_animals" title="Terrestrial animals">terrestrial animals</a> are not a <a href="/wiki/Monophyly" title="Monophyly">monophylytic group</a> -- that is, a group including all terrestrial animals does not encompass all lineages from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_ancestor" title="Common ancestor">common ancestor</a>. This is because there are organisms, such as the <a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">whale</a>, that evolved from terrestrial mammals <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_cetaceans" title="Evolution of cetaceans">back to an aquatic lifestyle</a>. Many <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a> of the past, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinosaurs" title="Dinosaurs">dinosaurs</a>, have become extinct due to extinction events, e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_extinction_event" title="Quaternary extinction event">Quaternary extinction event</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Land_surface_effects_on_climate" title="Land surface effects on climate">Land surface effects on climate</a></div>
<p>The land of Earth interacts with and influences <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> heavily since the land's surface heats up and cools down faster than air or water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PBS_Learning_Media_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-PBS_Learning_Media-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">Latitude</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elevation" title="Elevation">elevation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reflectivity" title="Reflectivity">reflectivity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">land use</a> all have varying effects. The latitude of the land will influence how much <a href="/wiki/Solar_irradiance" title="Solar irradiance">solar radiation</a> reaches the surface. High latitudes receive less solar radiation than low latitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PBS_Learning_Media_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-PBS_Learning_Media-84">[84]</a></sup> The land's topography is important in creating and transforming airflow and <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a> on Earth. Large landforms, such as mountain ranges, divert wind energy and make air parcels less dense and therefore able to hold less heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PBS_Learning_Media_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-PBS_Learning_Media-84">[84]</a></sup> As air rises, this cooling effect causes <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condensation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a>.
</p><p>The reflectivity of the earth is called <i>planetary <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a></i>, and the type of land cover that receives energy from the Sun affects the amount of energy that is reflected or transferred to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alan_Betts:_Atmospheric_Research_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alan_Betts:_Atmospheric_Research-85">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vegetation" title="Vegetation">Vegetation</a> has a relatively low albedo meaning that vegetated surfaces are good absorbers of the sun's energy. <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">Forests</a> have an albedo of 10–15% while <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grasslands</a> have an albedo of 15–20%. In comparison, sandy <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">deserts</a> have an albedo of 25–40%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alan_Betts:_Atmospheric_Research_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alan_Betts:_Atmospheric_Research-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Land use by humans also plays a role in the regional and global climate. Densely populated cities are warmer and create <a href="/wiki/Urban_heat_island" title="Urban heat island">urban heat islands</a> that have effects on the precipitation, cloud cover, and temperature of the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PBS_Learning_Media_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-PBS_Learning_Media-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Humans_and_land">Humans and land</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Humans and land">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2022</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_exploration">History of exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: History of exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Exploration" title="Exploration">Exploration</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_cartography" title="History of cartography">History of cartography</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Early_migrations_mercator.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="569" data-file-width="1173" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Early_migrations_mercator.svg/350px-Early_migrations_mercator.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Early_migrations_mercator.svg/525px-Early_migrations_mercator.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Early_migrations_mercator.svg/700px-Early_migrations_mercator.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Early_migrations_mercator.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overview map of the peopling of the world by early humans during the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a>, following to the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Dispersal" title="Southern Dispersal">Southern Dispersal</a> paradigm.</div></div></div>
<p>Two major eras of exploration occurred in human history: one of convergence, and one of divergence. The first, covering most of <i><a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">Homo sapiens</a></i> history, saw humans moving out of Africa, settling in new lands, and developing distinct cultures in relative isolation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-:12-86">[86]</a></sup> Early explorers settled in Europe and Asia; 14,000 years ago, some crossed the <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_the_Americas" title="Settlement of the Americas">Ice Age land bridge</a> from Siberia to Alaska, and moved southbound to settle in the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-:03-87">[87]</a></sup> For the most part, these cultures were ignorant of each other's existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-:12-86">[86]</a></sup> The second period of exploration, occurring over the last 10,000 years, saw increased cross-cultural exchange through trade and exploration, and marked a new era of cultural intermingling, and more recently, convergence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-:12-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early writings about exploration date back to the 4th millennium B.C. in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a>. One of the earliest and most impactful thinkers of exploration was <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> in 2nd century AD, Between the 5th century and 15th century AD, most exploration was done by Chinese and Arab explorers. This was followed by the European <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a> after European scholars rediscovered the works of early Latin and Greek geographers. While the Age of Discovery was partly driven by European land routes becoming unsafe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> and a desire for conquest, the 17th century saw exploration driven by nobler motives, including scientific discovery and the expansion of knowledge about the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-:03-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Travel">Travel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Travel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Travel" title="Travel">Travel</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%B5%D0%B7%D0%B4_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%8B_%D0%A8%D0%B0%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%89%D0%B5._%D0%92%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B6%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%82%D1%8C.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2433" data-file-width="3649" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%B5%D0%B7%D0%B4_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%8B_%D0%A8%D0%B0%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%89%D0%B5._%D0%92%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B6%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%82%D1%8C.jpg/220px-%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%B5%D0%B7%D0%B4_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%8B_%D0%A8%D0%B0%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%89%D0%B5._%D0%92%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B6%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%82%D1%8C.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%B5%D0%B7%D0%B4_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%8B_%D0%A8%D0%B0%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%89%D0%B5._%D0%92%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B6%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%82%D1%8C.jpg/330px-%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%B5%D0%B7%D0%B4_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%8B_%D0%A8%D0%B0%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%89%D0%B5._%D0%92%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B6%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%82%D1%8C.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%B5%D0%B7%D0%B4_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%8B_%D0%A8%D0%B0%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%89%D0%B5._%D0%92%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B6%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%82%D1%8C.jpg/440px-%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%B5%D0%B7%D0%B4_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%8B_%D0%A8%D0%B0%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%89%D0%B5._%D0%92%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B6%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%82%D1%8C.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%B5%D0%B7%D0%B4_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%8B_%D0%A8%D0%B0%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%89%D0%B5._%D0%92%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B6%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%82%D1%8C.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A train traveling across the <a href="/wiki/Voronezh_Oblast" title="Voronezh Oblast">Voronezh Oblast</a>, Russia</div></div></div>
<p>Humans have commonly traveled for business, pleasure, discovery, and adventure, all made easier in recent human history as a result of technologies like <a href="/wiki/Car" title="Car">cars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Train" title="Train">trains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Airplane" title="Airplane">planes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ship" title="Ship">ships</a>. <a href="/wiki/Land_navigation" title="Land navigation">Land navigation</a> is an aspect of travel and refers to progressing through unfamiliar terrain using navigational tools like maps with references to terrain, a <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compass</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Satellite_navigation" title="Satellite navigation">satellite navigation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hofman_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-hofman-89">[89]</a></sup> Navigation on land is often facilitated by reference to <a href="/wiki/Landmark" title="Landmark">landmarks</a> – enduring and recognizable natural or artificial features that stand out from their nearby environment that are often visible from long distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Natural landmarks can be characteristic features, such as <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountains</a> or <a href="/wiki/Plateau" title="Plateau">plateaus</a>, with examples including <a href="/wiki/Table_Mountain" title="Table Mountain">Table Mountain</a> in South Africa, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ararat" title="Mount Ararat">Mount Ararat</a> in Turkey, the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canyon" title="Grand Canyon">Grand Canyon</a> in the United States, <a href="/wiki/Uluru" title="Uluru">Uluru</a> in Australia, and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Fuji" title="Mount Fuji">Mount Fuji</a> in Japan. Landmarks and landforms may serve to attract <a href="/wiki/Tourism" title="Tourism">tourism</a>, whether <a href="/wiki/Domestic_tourism" title="Domestic tourism">domestic</a> (within the traveller's own country) or <a href="/wiki/International_tourism" title="International tourism">international</a>, and can generate important economic activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WTO2012Highlights_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-WTO2012Highlights-91">[91]</a></sup> Tourism based on a desire to experience aspects of land includes ecological tourism, or <a href="/wiki/Ecotourism" title="Ecotourism">ecotourism</a>, responsible travel to fragile, pristine, and usually protected areas that strives to be low-impact and (often) small-scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> It helps educate the traveller; provides funds for conservation; directly benefits the economic development and political empowerment of local communities, and fosters respect for different cultures and for human rights. <i>Take only memories and leave only footprints</i> is a very common slogan in protected areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">Migration</a> is the movement of people from one place to another with intentions of settling, permanently or temporarily, in a new country (external migration), or a new region of their country (<a href="/wiki/Internal_migration" title="Internal migration">internal migration</a>). Migration is often associated with better human capital at both individual and household levels, and with better access to migration networks, facilitating a possible second move. Age is also important for both work and non-work migration. People may migrate as individuals, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Family_unit" title="Family unit">family units</a> or in <a href="/wiki/Mass_migration" title="Mass migration">large groups</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade">Trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">Trade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_international_trade" title="Timeline of international trade">Timeline of international trade</a></div>
<p>Trade has occurred since the prehistoric era. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_Watson_(business_writer)" title="Peter Watson (business writer)">Peter Watson</a> dates the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_international_trade" title="History of international trade">history of long-distance commerce</a> from <abbr>c.</abbr> 150,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson2005_150_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson2005_150-94">[94]</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/Trade_route" title="Trade route">trade routes</a> throughout history have existed predominantly on land such as the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> which linked <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> with <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Amber_Road" title="Amber Road">Amber Road</a> which was used to transfer <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a> from <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern Europe</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_(historiography)" title="Dark Ages (historiography)">Dark Ages</a> led trade to collapse in the West, but it continued to flourish among the kingdoms of Africa, the Middle East, India, China, and Southeast Asia. During the Middle Ages, Central Asia was the economic centre of the world, and luxury goods were commonly traded in Europe. Physical money (either barter or precious metals) was dangerous to carry over a long distance. To address this, a burgeoning banking industry enabled the shift to movable wealth or capital, making it far easier and safer to trade across long distances. After the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">Age of Sail</a>, international trade mostly occurred along sea routes, notably to prevent intermediary countries from being able to control trade routes and the flow of goods.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_use">Land use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Land use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">Land use</a></div>
<p>Land use involves the management and modification of the <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">natural environment</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wilderness" title="Wilderness">wilderness</a> into <a href="/wiki/Built_environment" title="Built environment">built environment</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Human_settlement" title="Human settlement">settlements</a> and of semi-natural habitats such as <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable fields</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pastures</a>, and managed <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woods</a>. Land use by humans has a long history, first emerging more than 10,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> It has been defined as "the purposes and activities through which people interact with land and terrestrial ecosystems"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyfroidt_52–67_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meyfroidt_52–67-99">[99]</a></sup> and as "the total of arrangements, activities, and inputs that people undertake in a certain <a href="/wiki/Land_cover" title="Land cover">land</a> type."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Land use and <a href="/wiki/Land_management" title="Land management">land management</a> practices have a major impact on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_resources" title="Natural resources">natural resources</a> including <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Human tribes since <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a> have segregated land into <a href="/wiki/Territory" title="Territory">territories</a> to control the use of land. Today, the total <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a> is 10.7% of the land surface, with 1.3% being permanent cropland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">Agriculture</a> includes the farming of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crops" title="Crops">crops</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Office1999_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Office1999-104">[104]</a></sup> A third of Earth's land surface is used for agriculture,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbankagri_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-worldbankagri-14">[14]</a></sup> with estimated 16.7 million km<sup>2</sup> (6.4 million sq mi) of cropland and 33.5 million km<sup>2</sup> (12.9 million sq mi) of pastureland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hooke2012_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hooke2012-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_development">Urban development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Urban development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">Urban planning</a> is the design of all infrastructure in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urban_areas" title="Urban areas">urban areas</a> including <a href="/wiki/Housing" title="Housing">housing</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transportation" title="Transportation">transportation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wastewater" title="Wastewater">wastewater</a> systems. It also includes <a href="/wiki/Zoning" title="Zoning">zoning</a>, or the area of urban planning that is focused on dividing the land into zones, e.g. residential, commercial, and industrial zones.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earth_in_culture" title="Earth in culture">Earth in culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baylonianmaps.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2349" data-file-width="1728" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Baylonianmaps.JPG/220px-Baylonianmaps.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Baylonianmaps.JPG/330px-Baylonianmaps.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Baylonianmaps.JPG/440px-Baylonianmaps.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baylonianmaps.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Babylonian_Map_of_the_World" title="Babylonian Map of the World">Imago Mundi</a></i> Babylonian map, the oldest known world map, 6th century BC, <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Creation_myth" title="Creation myth">Creation myths</a> in many <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religions</a> recall a story involving the creation of the world by a supernatural <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a> or deities, including accounts wherein the land is separated from the oceans and the air. The Earth itself has often been personified as a <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a>, in particular a <a href="/wiki/Goddess" title="Goddess">goddess</a>. In many cultures, the <a href="/wiki/Mother_goddess" title="Mother goddess">mother goddess</a> is also portrayed as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_god" title="Fertility god">fertility deity</a>. To the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztecs</a>, Earth was called <i><a href="/wiki/Tonantzin" title="Tonantzin">Tonantzin</a></i>—"our mother"; to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incas" title="Incas">Incas</a>, Earth was called <i><a href="/wiki/Pachamama" title="Pachamama">Pachamama</a></i>—"mother earth". The Chinese Earth goddess <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hou_Tu" title="Hou Tu">Hou Tu</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> is similar to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaia_(mythology)" title="Gaia (mythology)">Gaia</a>, the Greek goddess personifying the Earth. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhumi_Devi" title="Bhumi Devi">Bhuma Devi</a> is the goddess of Earth in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> influenced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graha" title="Graha">Graha</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse mythology</a>, the Earth giantess <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6r%C3%B0" title="Jörð">Jörð</a> was the mother of <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a> and the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Annar" title="Annar">Annar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_religion" title="Ancient Egyptian religion">Ancient Egyptian mythology</a> is different from that of other cultures because Earth (<a href="/wiki/Geb" title="Geb">Geb</a>) is male and the sky (<a href="/wiki/Nut_(goddess)" title="Nut (goddess)">Nut</a>) is female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>In early Egyptian<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesopotamian_mythology" title="Mesopotamian mythology">Mesopotamian thought</a>, the world was portrayed as a flat disk floating in the ocean. The Egyptian universe was pictured as a rectangular box with a north–south orientation and a slightly concave surface, with Egypt in the centre. A similar model is found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeric" title="Homeric">Homeric</a> account of the 8th century BC in which "Okeanos, the personified body of water surrounding the circular surface of the Earth, is the begetter of all life and possibly of all gods."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Biblical_cosmology#Earth" title="Biblical cosmology">biblical earth</a> is also a flat disc floating on water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berlin_2011_189_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berlin_2011_189-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_Texts" title="Pyramid Texts">Pyramid Texts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coffin_Texts" title="Coffin Texts">Coffin Texts</a> reveal that the ancient Egyptians believed <a href="/wiki/Nu_(mythology)" title="Nu (mythology)">Nun</a> (the ocean) was a circular body surrounding <i>nbwt</i> (a term meaning "dry lands" or "islands"), and therefore believed in a similar Ancient Near Eastern circular Earth cosmography surrounded by water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>The spherical form of the Earth was suggested by early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophers" title="Greek philosophers">Greek philosophers</a>, a belief espoused by <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a>. Contrary to popular belief, most educated people in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> did not believe the Earth was flat: this misconception is often called the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myth_of_the_Flat_Earth" title="Myth of the Flat Earth">Myth of the Flat Earth</a>". As evidenced by thinkers such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, the European belief in a <a href="/wiki/Spherical_Earth" title="Spherical Earth">spherical Earth</a> was widespread by this point in time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Prior to <a href="/wiki/Magellan_expedition" title="Magellan expedition">circumnavigation of the planet</a> and the introduction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_flight" title="Space flight">space flight</a>, belief in a spherical Earth was based on observations of the secondary effects of the Earth's shape and parallels drawn with the shape of other planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflicts">Conflicts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Conflicts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Territorial_dispute" title="Territorial dispute">Territorial dispute</a></div>
<p>Many <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">wars</a> and other conflicts have occurred in efforts by participants to expand the land under their control, or to assert control of a specific area of land claimed to have historical or cultural significance to the participants. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> of the 13th and 14th centuries became the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_empires" title="List of largest empires">largest contiguous land empire</a> in <a href="/wiki/Human_history" title="Human history">history</a> through war and conquest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Originating in present-day <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>, the Mongol Empire at its height stretched from the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Japan" title="Sea of Japan">Sea of Japan</a> to parts of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, extending northward into parts of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> eastward and southward into parts of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, attempted invasions of <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> and conquered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_Plateau" title="Iranian Plateau">Iranian Plateau</a>; and westward as far as the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathian Mountains</a>.
</p><p>The aggression of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> was motivated in part by the concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Lebensraum" title="Lebensraum">Lebensraum</a></i> ("living space"), which had first became a geopolitical goal of <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Imperial Germany</a> in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> (1914–1918) originally, as the core element of the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Septemberprogramm" title="Septemberprogramm">Septemberprogramm</a></i></span> of territorial expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E/N301_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-E/N301-117">[117]</a></sup> The most extreme form of this ideology was supported by the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a> (NSDAP) and <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>. Lebensraum was one of the leading motivations Nazi Germany had in initiating <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, and it would continue this policy until the end of World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ref-3_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ref-3-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_issues">Environmental issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Environmental issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Human have a profound impact on the Earth's environment,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> with three-quarters of ice-free land having been modified through human habitation and land use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-12">[12]</a></sup> The major environmental issues related to land include protecting <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_ecosystem" title="Terrestrial ecosystem">terrestrial ecosystems</a>, preventing <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">biodiversity loss</a>, sustainable <a href="/wiki/Forest_management" title="Forest management">forest management</a>, combatting <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Waste_management" title="Waste management">waste management</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-122">[122]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Conservation_movement" title="Conservation movement">conservation movement</a> lobbies for the protection of <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered species</a> and the protection of <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">natural areas</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">Rising sea levels</a> as a result of <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> are expected to result in currently-inhabited land becoming partially submerged, resulting in mass migration and ecosystem loss. International frameworks have focused on analyzing how humans can meet their needs while using land more efficiently and preserving its natural resources, notably under the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>' <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">Sustainable Development Goals</a> framework.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution">Pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">Pollution</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ground">Ground</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Ground">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_pollution" title="Ground pollution">Ground pollution</a> includes <a href="/wiki/Litter" title="Litter">litter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soil_contamination" title="Soil contamination">soil contamination</a>. Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landfills" title="Landfills">landfills</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Apex_landfill" title="Apex landfill">Apex Regional landfill</a> in Las Vegas, can be thousands of acres in size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Air">Air</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Air">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%22When_the_smoke_came%22,_air_pollution_in_Shanghai.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2496" data-file-width="3729" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/%22When_the_smoke_came%22%2C_air_pollution_in_Shanghai.jpg/220px-%22When_the_smoke_came%22%2C_air_pollution_in_Shanghai.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/%22When_the_smoke_came%22%2C_air_pollution_in_Shanghai.jpg/330px-%22When_the_smoke_came%22%2C_air_pollution_in_Shanghai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/%22When_the_smoke_came%22%2C_air_pollution_in_Shanghai.jpg/440px-%22When_the_smoke_came%22%2C_air_pollution_in_Shanghai.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%22When_the_smoke_came%22,_air_pollution_in_Shanghai.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Air pollution in <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">Air pollution</a> on land can be caused by both human activities<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> (like travel) and natural phenomena. There are many different types of air pollutants, such as gases (including <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">nitrous oxides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbons" title="Chlorofluorocarbons">chlorofluorocarbons</a>), <a href="/wiki/Particulates" title="Particulates">particulates</a> (both organic and inorganic), and <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biological molecules</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manisalidis_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-manisalidis-125">[125]</a></sup> The main stationary air pollution sources include <a href="/wiki/Health_and_environmental_impact_of_the_coal_industry" title="Health and environmental impact of the coal industry">coal power plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_petroleum_industry" title="Environmental impact of the petroleum industry">oil refineries</a>, and incinerators. Air pollution is a significant <a href="/wiki/Risk_factor" title="Risk factor">risk factor</a> for many <a href="/wiki/List_of_pollution-related_diseases" title="List of pollution-related diseases">diseases</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_infection" title="Respiratory infection">respiratory infections</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_disease" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">COPD</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO20142_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO20142-126">[126]</a></sup> Growing evidence suggests that air pollution exposure may be associated with reduced IQ scores, impaired cognition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> increased risk for psychiatric disorders such as <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> and detrimental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perinatal" title="Perinatal">perinatal</a> health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1371/journal.pmed.1003718_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1371/journal.pmed.1003718-129">[129]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Smog" title="Smog">Smog</a> was a major issue in many countries during the 20th century; in London, the Great Smog of December 1952 killed nearly 12,000 people in 6 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EHP_112_1_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-EHP_112_1-130">[130]</a></sup> America produces roughly 25% of the world's <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO2</a>. In 2007, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">China</a> overtook the United States as the world's biggest producer of CO2, while still far behind based on per capita pollution (ranked 78th in the world).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Water">Water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polluted_Bagmati_River.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2985" data-file-width="3031" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Polluted_Bagmati_River.png/220px-Polluted_Bagmati_River.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Polluted_Bagmati_River.png/330px-Polluted_Bagmati_River.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Polluted_Bagmati_River.png/440px-Polluted_Bagmati_River.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polluted_Bagmati_River.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The polluted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bagmati_river" title="Bagmati river">Bagmati river</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">Water pollution</a> on land is the contamination of non-oceanic hydrological surface and underground water features such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lakes" title="Lakes">lakes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ponds" title="Ponds">ponds</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rivers" title="Rivers">rivers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Streams" title="Streams">streams</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wetlands" title="Wetlands">wetlands</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquifers" title="Aquifers">aquifers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reservoirs" title="Reservoirs">reservoirs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> as a result of human activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Von_Sperling3_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Von_Sperling3-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 6">: 6 </span></sup> It may be caused by toxic substances (e.g., oil, metals, plastics, <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutant" title="Persistent organic pollutant">persistent organic pollutants</a>, industrial waste products), stressful conditions (e.g., changes of pH, <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(environmental)" title="Hypoxia (environmental)">hypoxia</a> or anoxia, increased temperatures, excessive turbidity, unpleasant taste or odor, and changes of <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a>), or <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogenic organisms</a>. For example, releasing inadequately treated <a href="/wiki/Wastewater" title="Wastewater">wastewater</a> into natural waters can lead to the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">degradation</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecosystems" title="Aquatic ecosystems">aquatic ecosystems</a>. Excessive emissions of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulphur dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxide</a> can cause <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>, leading to soil degradation. <a href="/wiki/Groundwater_pollution" title="Groundwater pollution">Groundwater pollution</a> occurs when pollutants are released to the ground and make their way into aquifers; these aquifers may spread the contaminants over a large area, and lead to the contamination of <a href="/wiki/Well" title="Well">wells</a>, seeps and <a href="/wiki/Spring_(hydrology)" title="Spring (hydrology)">springs</a>, harming human and wildlife health.
</p><p>Water pollution reduces the ability of the body of water to provide the <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_service" title="Ecosystem service">ecosystem services</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">drinking water</a>) that it would otherwise provide, and can lead to <a href="/wiki/Waterborne_diseases" title="Waterborne diseases">water-borne diseases</a> for people using polluted water for drinking, bathing, washing or <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Control of water pollution requires appropriate <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a> and management plans as well as legislation. Technological solutions can include improving <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">sewage treatment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Industrial_wastewater_treatment" title="Industrial wastewater treatment">industrial wastewater treatment</a>, agricultural <a href="/wiki/Wastewater_treatment" title="Wastewater treatment">wastewater treatment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erosion_control" title="Erosion control">erosion control</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sediment_control" title="Sediment control">sediment control</a> and control of <a href="/wiki/Urban_runoff" title="Urban runoff">urban runoff</a> (including stormwater management).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity_loss">Biodiversity loss</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Biodiversity loss">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">Biodiversity loss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">Habitat destruction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red_List_Index,_OWID.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Red_List_Index%2C_OWID.svg/310px-Red_List_Index%2C_OWID.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Red_List_Index%2C_OWID.svg/465px-Red_List_Index%2C_OWID.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Red_List_Index%2C_OWID.svg/620px-Red_List_Index%2C_OWID.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red_List_Index,_OWID.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Red List Index of biodiversity (2019)</div></div></div>
<p>Terrestrial biodiversity loss refers to the worldwide extinction of various land-based <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, as well as the local reduction or loss of species in certain <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a>. Biodiversity loss can sometimes be reversed through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecological_restoration" title="Ecological restoration">ecological restoration</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ecological_resilience" title="Ecological resilience">ecological resilience</a>; however, it may also be permanent (e.g. through <a href="/wiki/Land_loss" title="Land loss">land loss</a>). The planet's ecosystem is quite sensitive: occasionally, minor changes from a healthy <a href="/wiki/Equilibrium_point" title="Equilibrium point">equilibrium</a> can have dramatic influence on a <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">food web</a> or <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a>, up to and including the <a href="/wiki/Coextinction" title="Coextinction">coextinction</a> of that entire food chain. Biodiversity loss leads to reduced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a>, and can eventually threaten <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cardinale20122_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-cardinale20122-133">[133]</a></sup> Earth is currently undergoing its <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">sixth mass extinction</a> (the <i>Holocene extinction</i>) as a result of human activities which push beyond the <a href="/wiki/Planetary_boundaries" title="Planetary boundaries">planetary boundaries</a>. So far, this extinction has proven irreversible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GhastlyFuture2_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-GhastlyFuture2-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>World leaders and supranational organizations are increasingly focused on addressing biodiversity loss. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_Biological_Diversity" title="Convention on Biological Diversity">UN Convention on Biological Diversity</a> is a treaty meant to biodiversity loss and proactively <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conserving</a> wild areas. The fifteenth <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">UN SDG</a> goal (<a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_15" title="Sustainable Development Goal 15">"Life on land"</a>) proposes 12 targets to be reached by 2030 and 14 indicators to measure progress, with annual updates delivered by the UN Secretary-General. In the 2022 update, the Secretary notes that deforestation targets have not been met, that the Red List Index has worsened by 10% over the past 3 decades, and that only a third of countries are on track to reach or exceed their national biodiversity targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_degradation">Land degradation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Land degradation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">Land degradation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_soil_degradation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="388" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/World_soil_degradation.jpg/310px-World_soil_degradation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/World_soil_degradation.jpg/465px-World_soil_degradation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/World_soil_degradation.jpg/620px-World_soil_degradation.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_soil_degradation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World map of soil degradation</div></div></div>
<p>Human-induced processes have led to biophysical <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">degradation</a> of wide swaths of land; these processes include human actions that can indirectly affect phenomena such as floods and bushfires.
</p><p>Land degradation is considered an important topic of the 21st century due to its implications for <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_productivity" title="Agricultural productivity">agricultural productivity</a>, the environment, and <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>. It is estimated that up to 4% of the world's agricultural land is seriously degraded.
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Special_Report_on_Climate_Change_and_Land" title="Special Report on Climate Change and Land">Special Report on Climate Change and Land</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a>: "About a quarter of the Earth's ice-free land area is subject to human-induced degradation (medium confidence). <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">Soil erosion</a> from agricultural fields is estimated to be currently 10 to 20 times (no-tillage) to more than 100 times (conventional tillage) higher than the soil formation rate (medium confidence)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United Nations estimates that about 30% of land is degraded worldwide, and about 3.2 billion people reside in these degrading areas. About 12 million hectares of productive land – roughly equal to the size of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> – is degraded every year. This happens because people exploit the land without protecting it. The United Nations <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_15" title="Sustainable Development Goal 15">Sustainable Development Goal 15</a> has a target to restore degraded land and soil and achieve a land degradation-neutral world by 2030 through <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable agriculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Desertification">Desertification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Desertification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">Desertification</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Desertification_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4400" data-file-width="6800" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Desertification_map.png/310px-Desertification_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Desertification_map.png/465px-Desertification_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Desertification_map.png/620px-Desertification_map.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Desertification_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. Department of Agriculture map from 1998 showing global desertification vulnerability</div></div></div>
<p>Desertification is a type of land degradation in <a href="/wiki/Drylands" title="Drylands">drylands</a> in which fertile areas become increasingly arid as a result of natural processes or human activities, resulting in loss of biological productivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> This spread of arid areas can be caused by a variety of factors, such as <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeng_L17401_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zeng_L17401-141">[141]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Overgrazing" title="Overgrazing">overgrazing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Overexploitation" title="Overexploitation">overexploitation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> as a result of human activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Throughout geological history, deserts have occurred naturally. In recent times, the potential influences of human activity, improper land management, deforestation and climate change on desertification is the subject of many scientific investigations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Land&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Solid_earth" title="Solid earth">Solid earth</a></li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-NatlGeo-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-NatlGeo_24-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190716045120/https://www.nationalgeographic.org/encyclopedia/continent/" rel="nofollow">"How many continents are there?"</a>. <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic Society</a>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">July 27,</span> 2017</span>. <q>By convention there are seven continents: <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>. Some geographers list only six continents, combining <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> into Eurasia. In parts of the world, students learn that there are just five continents: Eurasia, Australia (Oceania), Africa, Antarctica, and the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=How+many+continents+are+there%3F&amp;rft.pub=National+Geographic+Society&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.nationalgeographic.org%2Fencyclopedia%2Fcontinent%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALand"></span></span>
